### Introduction



In this chapter, we will explore the role of noble gases in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Noble gases, also known as inert gases, are a group of chemical elements that are characterized by their low reactivity and abundance in the Earth's atmosphere. These elements include helium, neon, argon, krypton, xenon, and radon. While they may seem insignificant, noble gases play a crucial role in understanding the formation and evolution of our planet.



Throughout this chapter, we will discuss the various ways in which noble gases provide valuable insights into the Earth's history. We will start by examining the abundance and distribution of noble gases in different layers of the Earth, including the atmosphere, crust, mantle, and core. We will then delve into the processes that have influenced the distribution of noble gases, such as volcanic activity, plate tectonics, and atmospheric escape.



One of the most significant contributions of noble gases to our understanding of the Earth's evolution is their use as tracers of ancient processes. By analyzing the isotopic ratios of noble gases, scientists can determine the age of rocks and minerals, providing valuable information about the timing of geological events. We will explore the various techniques used to date rocks and minerals using noble gases and discuss their limitations and applications.



Furthermore, noble gases can also provide insights into the composition and origin of the Earth. By studying the isotopic ratios of noble gases, scientists can determine the source of different elements and their contribution to the Earth's composition. We will discuss the various theories and models proposed to explain the origin of noble gases and their implications for the formation and evolution of our planet.



In conclusion, this chapter will provide a comprehensive exploration of the role of noble gases in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By the end, readers will have a better understanding of the significance of these seemingly inert elements and their contributions to our understanding of the history of our planet. 





## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.1 Mantle Structure and Noble Gas Distribution:



The Earth's mantle is a layer of the planet that lies between the crust and the core. It is the largest layer of the Earth, making up about 84% of its volume. The mantle is composed of solid rock, but it is capable of flowing over long periods of time due to its high temperature and pressure. The mantle is divided into two main regions: the upper mantle and the lower mantle. The upper mantle is further divided into the lithosphere and the asthenosphere.



The lithosphere is the rigid outer layer of the Earth, consisting of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle. It is about 100 km thick and is divided into several tectonic plates that move and interact with each other. The asthenosphere, on the other hand, is a more ductile layer of the upper mantle that lies beneath the lithosphere. It is characterized by its ability to flow and deform, allowing the tectonic plates to move on top of it.



The distribution of noble gases in the mantle is not uniform. The upper mantle has a higher concentration of noble gases compared to the lower mantle. This is due to the fact that the upper mantle is closer to the Earth's surface, where noble gases are more abundant. The lower mantle, on the other hand, is deeper and has a higher pressure and temperature, which can cause noble gases to be incorporated into minerals and become less mobile.



### Subsection: 5.1a Overview of Mantle Structure



The structure of the mantle is crucial in understanding the distribution of noble gases. The lithosphere, being the uppermost layer of the mantle, has a significant influence on the noble gas budget of the Earth. The lithosphere is composed of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle, which have different compositions and physical properties.



The crust is the thinnest layer of the Earth, with an average thickness of about 35 km. It is composed of various types of rocks, including igneous, sedimentary, and metamorphic rocks. The crust has a lower concentration of noble gases compared to the upper mantle, as it is closer to the Earth's surface and has been exposed to atmospheric processes.



The uppermost part of the mantle, known as the upper mantle, is composed of peridotite, a rock rich in iron and magnesium. This layer has a higher concentration of noble gases compared to the crust, as it is closer to the Earth's interior and has not been exposed to atmospheric processes. The upper mantle also has a higher temperature and pressure, which can cause noble gases to be incorporated into minerals and become less mobile.



The asthenosphere, which lies beneath the lithosphere, is a more ductile layer of the upper mantle. It is characterized by its ability to flow and deform, allowing the tectonic plates to move on top of it. The asthenosphere has a lower concentration of noble gases compared to the upper mantle, as it is deeper and has a higher temperature and pressure, which can cause noble gases to be incorporated into minerals and become less mobile.



In conclusion, the structure of the mantle plays a crucial role in the distribution of noble gases. The lithosphere, composed of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle, has a higher concentration of noble gases compared to the asthenosphere, which lies beneath it. This distribution is influenced by factors such as depth, temperature, and pressure, which can affect the mobility and incorporation of noble gases into minerals. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.1 Mantle Structure and Noble Gas Distribution:



The Earth's mantle is a crucial layer in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. It is a layer of solid rock that lies between the crust and the core, making up about 84% of the Earth's volume. The mantle is divided into two main regions: the upper mantle and the lower mantle. The upper mantle is further divided into the lithosphere and the asthenosphere.



The lithosphere is the rigid outer layer of the Earth, consisting of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle. It is about 100 km thick and is divided into several tectonic plates that move and interact with each other. The lithosphere plays a significant role in the distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle. The crust, being the uppermost layer of the lithosphere, has a significant influence on the noble gas budget of the Earth.



The asthenosphere, on the other hand, is a more ductile layer of the upper mantle that lies beneath the lithosphere. It is characterized by its ability to flow and deform, allowing the tectonic plates to move on top of it. The asthenosphere also plays a role in the distribution of noble gases, as it is a more mobile layer compared to the lithosphere.



### Subsection: 5.1a Overview of Mantle Structure



The structure of the mantle is crucial in understanding the distribution of noble gases. The lithosphere, being the uppermost layer of the mantle, has a significant influence on the noble gas budget of the Earth. The lithosphere is composed of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle, which have different compositions and physical properties.



The crust is the thinnest layer of the Earth, with an average thickness of about 35 km. It is composed of various types of rocks, including igneous, sedimentary, and metamorphic rocks. The composition of the crust varies depending on its location, but it is generally enriched in silica and aluminum compared to the mantle. This difference in composition can affect the distribution of noble gases, as some noble gases, such as helium, are more abundant in the crust due to their production from the decay of radioactive elements.



The uppermost part of the mantle, also known as the upper mantle, is composed of peridotite, a rock rich in iron and magnesium. This composition is significantly different from the crust, and it is also the source of most of the Earth's volcanic activity. The upper mantle is also where the asthenosphere is located, a more ductile layer that can flow and deform. This layer plays a crucial role in the distribution of noble gases, as it can transport them from the lower mantle to the surface through volcanic activity.



The lower mantle is the deepest layer of the mantle, extending from 660 km to the core-mantle boundary at 2900 km. It is composed of a dense rock called perovskite, which is rich in iron and magnesium. The lower mantle has a higher pressure and temperature compared to the upper mantle, which can affect the distribution of noble gases. At these high pressures and temperatures, noble gases can be incorporated into minerals and become less mobile, leading to a lower concentration of noble gases in the lower mantle compared to the upper mantle.



### Subsection: 5.1b Noble Gas Distribution in the Mantle



The distribution of noble gases in the mantle is not uniform. The upper mantle has a higher concentration of noble gases compared to the lower mantle. This is due to the fact that the upper mantle is closer to the Earth's surface, where noble gases are more abundant. The lower mantle, on the other hand, is deeper and has a higher pressure and temperature, which can cause noble gases to be incorporated into minerals and become less mobile.



The distribution of noble gases in the mantle is also affected by the process of mantle convection. Mantle convection is the process by which heat is transferred from the Earth's core to the surface, causing the mantle to flow and deform. This process can transport noble gases from the lower mantle to the upper mantle, where they can be released through volcanic activity.



The Iceland hotspot is an example of how mantle convection can affect the distribution of noble gases. The Iceland hotspot is a region of high volcanic activity located in the North Atlantic Ocean. It is believed to be caused by a plume of hot material rising from the lower mantle. This plume can transport noble gases from the lower mantle to the surface, leading to a higher concentration of noble gases in the volcanic activity in Iceland.



In conclusion, the distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle is not uniform and is influenced by various factors such as the composition and structure of the mantle, mantle convection, and volcanic activity. Further research and exploration of the Earth's mantle are necessary to fully understand the distribution and role of noble gases in the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.1 Mantle Structure and Noble Gas Distribution:



The Earth's mantle is a crucial layer in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. It is a layer of solid rock that lies between the crust and the core, making up about 84% of the Earth's volume. The mantle is divided into two main regions: the upper mantle and the lower mantle. The upper mantle is further divided into the lithosphere and the asthenosphere.



The lithosphere is the rigid outer layer of the Earth, consisting of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle. It is about 100 km thick and is divided into several tectonic plates that move and interact with each other. The lithosphere plays a significant role in the distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle. The crust, being the uppermost layer of the lithosphere, has a significant influence on the noble gas budget of the Earth.



The asthenosphere, on the other hand, is a more ductile layer of the upper mantle that lies beneath the lithosphere. It is characterized by its ability to flow and deform, allowing the tectonic plates to move on top of it. The asthenosphere also plays a role in the distribution of noble gases, as it is a more mobile layer compared to the lithosphere.



### Subsection: 5.1a Overview of Mantle Structure



The structure of the mantle is crucial in understanding the distribution of noble gases. The lithosphere, being the uppermost layer of the mantle, has a significant influence on the noble gas budget of the Earth. The lithosphere is composed of the crust and the uppermost part of the mantle, which have different compositions and physical properties.



The crust is the thinnest layer of the Earth, with an average thickness of about 35 km. It is composed of various types of rocks, including igneous, sedimentary, and metamorphic rocks. The composition of the crust varies depending on its location, but it is primarily made up of oxygen, silicon, aluminum, iron, calcium, sodium, and potassium. These elements are also important in the distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle.



The uppermost part of the mantle, also known as the upper mantle, is composed of peridotite, a rock rich in iron and magnesium. This composition is significantly different from that of the crust, and it plays a crucial role in the distribution of noble gases. The upper mantle is also where most of the Earth's mantle convection occurs, which is an important process in the transport of noble gases.



### Subsection: 5.1b Noble Gas Distribution in the Mantle



The distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle is not uniform. The lithosphere, asthenosphere, and upper mantle have different noble gas concentrations due to their varying compositions and physical properties. The crust, being the uppermost layer, has the highest concentration of noble gases, while the asthenosphere has the lowest.



The crust, being primarily composed of oxygen, silicon, and aluminum, has a high concentration of noble gases such as helium, neon, and argon. These gases are produced through the decay of radioactive elements such as uranium and thorium, which are abundant in the Earth's crust. The crust also acts as a barrier, preventing noble gases from escaping into the atmosphere.



The upper mantle, on the other hand, has a lower concentration of noble gases compared to the crust. This is due to the composition of the upper mantle, which is rich in iron and magnesium. These elements do not produce noble gases through radioactive decay, resulting in a lower concentration. However, the upper mantle is where most of the Earth's mantle convection occurs, which can transport noble gases from the lower mantle to the crust.



The asthenosphere, being a more mobile layer, has the lowest concentration of noble gases. This is because the asthenosphere is constantly moving and mixing with the upper mantle, resulting in a dilution of noble gases. However, the asthenosphere can also act as a reservoir for noble gases, storing them for long periods of time before they are released through volcanic activity.



### Subsection: 5.1c Implications of Noble Gas Distribution



The distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle has significant implications for our understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. The high concentration of noble gases in the crust suggests that the Earth's crust has been relatively stable over time, with minimal mixing from the lower mantle. This stability is crucial in maintaining the Earth's habitable conditions.



The lower concentration of noble gases in the upper mantle and asthenosphere suggests that these layers have been more active and dynamic, with constant mixing and transport of materials. This is supported by evidence from mantle convection and plate tectonics, which play a crucial role in the distribution of noble gases.



Furthermore, the distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle can also provide insights into the origin and evolution of our planet. By studying the isotopic ratios of noble gases, scientists can trace the origins of different layers of the Earth and understand how they have evolved over time.



In conclusion, the distribution of noble gases in the Earth's mantle is a complex and dynamic process that is influenced by the composition and physical properties of different layers. By studying this distribution, we can gain a better understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet and its origins. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.2 Isotope Geochemistry of Noble Gases in the Mantle:



### Subsection: 5.2a Basics of Isotope Geochemistry



Isotope geochemistry is a powerful tool for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It involves the study of the relative abundances of different isotopes of elements in various chemical pools, such as rocks, minerals, and fluids. In this section, we will discuss the basics of isotope geochemistry and its application to the study of noble gases in the Earth's mantle.



#### Stable vs radioactive isotopes



Isotopes are atoms of the same element that have different numbers of neutrons in their nucleus. Some isotopes are stable and do not undergo any radioactive decay, while others are unstable and decay into other elements over time. The element hydrogen, for example, has three naturally occurring isotopes: <sup>1</sup>H, <sup>2</sup>H, and <sup>3</sup>H, which are also known as protium, deuterium, and tritium, respectively. While <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>2</sup>H are stable, <sup>3</sup>H is unstable and decays into <sup>3</sup>He.



#### Isotope notation



Isotope notation is used to represent the relative abundance of different isotopes in a chemical pool. It consists of the element's symbol, followed by the mass number (the sum of protons and neutrons in the nucleus) as a superscript and the atomic number (the number of protons in the nucleus) as a subscript. For example, the isotope notation for hydrogen is <sup>1</sup>H, <sup>2</sup>H, and <sup>3</sup>H for protium, deuterium, and tritium, respectively.



#### Isotope fractionation



Isotope fractionation is the process by which the relative abundance of different isotopes changes due to physical or chemical processes. This can occur during phase changes, such as evaporation or condensation, or during chemical reactions. Isotope fractionation can also occur due to differences in the mass of isotopes, known as mass-dependent fractionation, or due to differences in the chemical properties of isotopes, known as mass-independent fractionation.



#### Isotope geochemistry of noble gases in the mantle



The Earth's mantle is a crucial layer in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. It is composed of various minerals, including olivine, pyroxene, and garnet, which have different isotopic compositions of noble gases. The distribution of noble gases in the mantle is influenced by processes such as degassing, subduction, and mantle convection.



Studies have shown that the noble gas isotopic composition of the mantle is similar to that of meteorites, indicating a common origin. However, there are variations in the isotopic composition of noble gases between different regions of the mantle, which can provide insights into the processes that have shaped the Earth's mantle over time.



#### Conclusion



In this section, we have discussed the basics of isotope geochemistry and its application to the study of noble gases in the Earth's mantle. Isotope geochemistry has provided valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet, and ongoing research in this field continues to expand our understanding of the Earth's history. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the isotope geochemistry of noble gases in the mantle and its implications for the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.2 Isotope Geochemistry of Noble Gases in the Mantle:



### Subsection: 5.2b Isotope Geochemistry of Noble Gases in the Mantle



Isotope geochemistry is a powerful tool for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It involves the study of the relative abundances of different isotopes of elements in various chemical pools, such as rocks, minerals, and fluids. In this section, we will focus on the application of isotope geochemistry to the study of noble gases in the Earth's mantle.



#### Noble gas isotopes



Noble gases, also known as inert gases, are a group of elements that are chemically inert and do not readily form compounds with other elements. These elements include helium (He), neon (Ne), argon (Ar), krypton (Kr), xenon (Xe), and radon (Rn). These elements have multiple stable isotopes, with the exception of radon, which is a radioactive isotope.



#### Radiogenic and nucleogenic production processes



Natural isotopic variations amongst the noble gases result from both radiogenic and nucleogenic production processes. Radiogenic production occurs through the decay of radioactive elements, such as uranium and thorium, which produce helium and argon isotopes. Nucleogenic production, on the other hand, occurs through nuclear reactions induced by cosmic rays or high-energy particles, which produce helium and neon isotopes.



#### Helium-3 in the Earth's mantle



Helium-3 (3He) is a rare isotope of helium that was trapped in the planet when it formed. Some 3He is being added by meteoric dust, primarily collecting on the bottom of oceans. However, due to subduction, all oceanic tectonic plates are younger than continental plates, and 3He will be degassed from oceanic sediment during subduction. This means that cosmogenic 3He is not affecting the concentration or noble gas ratios of the mantle.



#### Lithium spallation and helium-3 production



Lithium spallation is the process by which a high-energy neutron bombards a lithium atom, creating a 3He and a 4He ion. This process generally occurs in the crust and contributes to the production of 3He in the Earth's mantle. However, significant amounts of lithium are required to affect the 3He/4He ratio in the mantle.



#### Helium-3 as a marker of deep origin



It has been observed that 3He is present in volcano emissions and oceanic ridge samples. This suggests that 3He is stored in the planet's mantle and is used as a marker of material of deep origin. The exact mechanism of how 3He is stored in the mantle is still under investigation.



#### Helium-4 in the Earth's mantle



Helium-4 (4He) is created by radiogenic production through the decay of uranium and thorium. This process contributes to the overall helium content in the Earth's mantle. However, all degassed helium is eventually lost to space due to the average speed of helium exceeding the escape velocity for the Earth. This means that the helium content and ratios of Earth's atmosphere have remained stable over time.



#### Outgassing of helium and volatile components



Due to similarities in helium and carbon in magma chemistry, outgassing of helium requires the loss of volatile components, such as water and carbon dioxide, from the mantle. This process typically occurs at depths of less than 60 km. However, 3He is primarily transported to the surface trapped in the crystal lattice of minerals within fluid inclusions.



In summary, the study of noble gas isotopes in the Earth's mantle provides valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. By understanding the production processes and storage mechanisms of noble gases, we can better understand the composition and history of the Earth's mantle. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.2 Isotope Geochemistry of Noble Gases in the Mantle:



### Subsection: 5.2c Case Studies of Isotope Geochemistry



In addition to the general principles of isotope geochemistry discussed in the previous subsection, case studies of isotope geochemistry in the Earth's mantle provide valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. These studies involve the analysis of noble gas isotopes in various geological settings, such as mid-ocean ridges, ore deposits, and hydrothermal systems.



#### Mid-ocean ridges



Isotopic data from various locations within the Mid-Atlantic Ridge have revealed the presence of mafic-to-felsic intrusive igneous rocks, including gabbro, diorite, and plagiogranite. These rocks have undergone high-grade metamorphism due to the presence of magmatic water, with temperatures exceeding 600°C. This deformation has resulted in the depletion of <sup>18</sup>O in the host rocks, leading to further analysis of the ratio of <sup>18</sup>O to <sup>16</sup>O (δ<sup>18</sup>O).



Interestingly, the isotopic signature of magmatic water in these rocks shows similarities to meteoric water, indicating the circulation of both magmatic and meteoric groundwater systems. This suggests that the Earth's mantle has been influenced by external water sources, which has implications for the thermal and chemical evolution of the planet.



#### Ore deposits



Isotopic analyses of fluid inclusions within ore deposits have also provided valuable insights into the noble gas isotopic composition of the Earth's mantle. These studies have shown a wide range of δ<sup>18</sup>O and δ<sup>2</sup>H content, with an abundance of <sup>18</sup>O and depletion in <sup>2</sup>H relative to SMOW and meteoric waters. This suggests that the fluids within these environments have interacted with the mantle, resulting in the incorporation of mantle-derived noble gases.



#### Hydrothermal systems



Hydrothermal systems, which involve the circulation of hot fluids through the Earth's crust, also provide a unique opportunity to study the isotopic composition of noble gases in the mantle. These systems are characterized by rapid exchange of hydrogen atoms with electronegative bonds, such as O-H, N-H, and S-H, which can complicate the preservation of information in isotope studies.



Despite these challenges, studies have shown that the hydrogen isotopic composition of hydrothermal fluids can provide valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. By understanding the rates of hydrogen exchange and the origin of the hydrogen moieties in studied molecules, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet over time.



In conclusion, case studies of isotope geochemistry in the Earth's mantle have provided valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. By analyzing noble gas isotopes in various geological settings, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that have shaped the Earth and continue to shape it today. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.3 Mantle Plumes and their Noble Gas Signatures:



### Subsection (optional): 5.3a Understanding Mantle Plumes



Mantle plumes are one of the most intriguing and debated topics in geodynamics. These are thought to be narrow, cylindrical columns of hot, buoyant material that rise from the core-mantle boundary to the Earth's surface. The concept of mantle plumes was first proposed by Jason Morgan in 1971 to explain the formation of the Hawaiian Islands and other volcanic hotspots. Since then, numerous studies have been conducted to understand the origin, behavior, and geochemical signatures of mantle plumes.



One of the key methods used to study mantle plumes is through the analysis of noble gas isotopes. Noble gases, such as helium, neon, argon, krypton, and xenon, are chemically inert and do not react with other elements. This makes them ideal tracers for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. The isotopic composition of noble gases can provide valuable insights into the origin and source of mantle plumes, as well as the processes they undergo during their ascent to the surface.



The chemistry, heat flow, and melting of mantle plumes are closely linked to their noble gas signatures. The chemical and isotopic composition of basalts found at hotspots, also known as ocean island basalts (OIBs), differ from mid-ocean ridge basalts (MORBs). This difference is attributed to the mixing of near-surface materials, such as subducted slabs and continental sediments, in the mantle source. The geochemical signature of OIBs is characterized by the presence of mantle components, including DMM (depleted mid-ocean ridge basalt mantle), HIMU (high U/Pb-ratio mantle), EM1 (enriched mantle 1), EM2 (enriched mantle 2), and FOZO (focus zone). These components are thought to arise from the mixing of near-surface materials that have been transported down to the core-mantle boundary by subducting slabs and then brought back up to the surface by mantle plumes.



Stable isotopes, such as iron, can also be used to track the processes that mantle plumes undergo during melting. The processing of oceanic crust and sediments by mantle plumes can result in the depletion of certain isotopes, providing evidence for the involvement of these materials in the plume's source.



One of the most well-studied examples of a mantle plume is the Iceland hotspot. This hotspot is located at the boundary between the North American and Eurasian plates and has been active for over 60 million years. The geochemical signature of the Iceland hotspot is characterized by a high abundance of helium-3, which is thought to be derived from the Earth's primordial mantle. This suggests that the Iceland hotspot is tapping into a deep, primitive reservoir within the Earth's mantle.



Geodynamic models have also been used to explain the observations of mantle plumes. These models aim to resolve the paradox of a broad, relatively cool plume being in better agreement with observations than a thin, hot plume. The most recent models suggest that mantle plumes are around 180-200°C hotter than the surrounding mantle and have a stem with a radius of approximately 100 km. However, these temperatures have not yet been confirmed by petrology, highlighting the need for further research and exploration.



In conclusion, the study of noble gas isotopes has provided valuable insights into the understanding of mantle plumes and their role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By analyzing the geochemical signatures of mantle plumes, we can gain a better understanding of their origin, source, and processes they undergo during their ascent to the surface. Further research and exploration in this field will continue to enhance our understanding of the Earth's dynamic interior.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.3 Mantle Plumes and their Noble Gas Signatures:



### Subsection (optional): 5.3b Noble Gas Signatures of Mantle Plumes



Mantle plumes are a fascinating and highly debated topic in geodynamics. These narrow, cylindrical columns of hot, buoyant material are thought to rise from the core-mantle boundary to the Earth's surface, playing a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. The concept of mantle plumes was first proposed by Jason Morgan in 1971 to explain the formation of the Hawaiian Islands and other volcanic hotspots. Since then, numerous studies have been conducted to understand the origin, behavior, and geochemical signatures of mantle plumes.



One of the key methods used to study mantle plumes is through the analysis of noble gas isotopes. Noble gases, such as helium, neon, argon, krypton, and xenon, are chemically inert and do not react with other elements. This makes them ideal tracers for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. The isotopic composition of noble gases can provide valuable insights into the origin and source of mantle plumes, as well as the processes they undergo during their ascent to the surface.



The chemistry, heat flow, and melting of mantle plumes are closely linked to their noble gas signatures. The chemical and isotopic composition of basalts found at hotspots, also known as ocean island basalts (OIBs), differ from mid-ocean ridge basalts (MORBs). This difference is attributed to the mixing of near-surface materials, such as subducted slabs and continental sediments, in the mantle source. The geochemical signature of OIBs is characterized by the presence of mantle components, including DMM (depleted mid-ocean ridge basalt mantle), HIMU (high U/Pb-ratio mantle), EM1 (enriched mantle 1), EM2 (enriched mantle 2), and FOZO (focus zone). These components are thought to arise from the mixing of near-surface materials that have been transported down to the core-mantle boundary by subducting slabs and then brought back up to the surface by mantle plumes.



Stable isotopes, such as iron, can also be used to track the processes that the uprising material experiences during melting. The processing of oceanic crust, lithosphere, and sediment through a subduction zone decouples the water-soluble trace elements (e.g., K, Rb, Th) from the immobile trace elements (e.g., Ti, Nb, Ta), concentrating the immobile elements in the oceanic slab. This process is important in understanding the noble gas signatures of mantle plumes, as the water-soluble elements are added to the crust in island arc volcanoes.



Seismic tomography has shown that subducted oceanic slabs can sink as far as the bottom of the mantle transition zone at 650 km depth. While subduction to greater depths is less certain, there is evidence that they may sink to mid-lower-mantle depths at about 1,500 km depth. This is significant in understanding the source of mantle plumes, which is postulated to be the core-mantle boundary at 3,000 km depth.



In conclusion, the noble gas signatures of mantle plumes provide valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. By studying these signatures, we can better understand the origin and behavior of mantle plumes, and their role in shaping our planet's history. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.3 Mantle Plumes and their Noble Gas Signatures:



### Subsection (optional): 5.3c Implications of Noble Gas Signatures



The study of noble gas isotopes in mantle plumes has provided valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. These isotopes, which are chemically inert and do not react with other elements, act as tracers for understanding the processes that have shaped our planet. In this section, we will explore the implications of noble gas signatures in mantle plumes and how they contribute to our understanding of the Earth's evolution.



One of the key implications of noble gas signatures in mantle plumes is the identification of different mantle components. As mentioned in the previous section, the geochemical signature of ocean island basalts (OIBs) is characterized by the presence of various mantle components, including DMM, HIMU, EM1, EM2, and FOZO. These components have distinct noble gas signatures, which can be used to trace their origin and source in the mantle. For example, the presence of high helium-3 to helium-4 ratios in OIBs is indicative of a deep mantle source, while low helium-3 to helium-4 ratios suggest a shallow mantle source.



Furthermore, the isotopic composition of noble gases can also provide information about the melting and mixing processes that occur in mantle plumes. The presence of different noble gas isotopes in OIBs compared to mid-ocean ridge basalts (MORBs) is evidence of the mixing of near-surface materials in the mantle source. This mixing is thought to occur as the mantle plume ascends towards the surface, incorporating subducted slabs and continental sediments along the way. The resulting geochemical signature of OIBs is a combination of these different mantle components, providing a unique insight into the processes that have shaped the Earth's mantle.



Another implication of noble gas signatures in mantle plumes is their role in understanding the thermal evolution of the Earth. The isotopic composition of noble gases can be used to estimate the temperature of the mantle source from which they originated. This is because the isotopic ratios of noble gases are sensitive to temperature, with heavier isotopes being more abundant at higher temperatures. By analyzing the noble gas signatures of mantle plumes, we can gain a better understanding of the thermal history of the Earth and how it has evolved over time.



In addition to providing insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth, noble gas signatures in mantle plumes also have implications for the formation of other celestial bodies. The study of noble gases in meteorites and other planetary materials has revealed similarities in their isotopic compositions to those found in mantle plumes. This suggests that the processes that have shaped the Earth's mantle may also be at play in the formation of other planets and bodies in our solar system.



In conclusion, the study of noble gas signatures in mantle plumes has greatly contributed to our understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. These signatures provide valuable insights into the origin and source of mantle plumes, the processes they undergo during their ascent, and the thermal history of our planet. Furthermore, they have implications for the formation of other celestial bodies, expanding our knowledge of the universe beyond our own planet. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.4 Noble Gas Diffusion and its Implications for Mantle Dynamics:



### Subsection (optional): 5.4a Basics of Noble Gas Diffusion



Noble gas diffusion is a process that plays a crucial role in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. As mentioned in the previous section, noble gases are chemically inert and do not react with other elements, making them ideal tracers for studying the processes that have shaped our planet. In this section, we will explore the basics of noble gas diffusion and its implications for mantle dynamics.



In chemical physics, atomic diffusion is the process by which atoms in a solid move randomly and result in the net transport of atoms. This process is governed by the diffusivity and the concentration gradient, and is influenced by factors such as temperature and the structure of the crystal lattice. In the crystal solid state, diffusion can occur through either interstitial or substitutional mechanisms, known as lattice diffusion. In interstitial lattice diffusion, atoms diffuse in between the lattice structure of another crystalline element, while in substitutional lattice diffusion, atoms can only move by substituting places with another atom. The prevalence of point vacancies in the crystal lattice, which increases with temperature according to the Arrhenius equation, also affects the rate of diffusion.



For noble gases, diffusion occurs through the process of jump diffusion, where the atoms migrate from point vacancy to point vacancy by rapid, essentially random jumping. This movement can be described by the "random walk" model, where after a certain number of jumps, the atom will have moved an average distance. The frequency of jumps, denoted by T, is measured in jumps per second, and the average distance moved after n jumps of length α can be calculated using the equation:



$$

\Delta x = \sqrt{n}\alpha

$$



This equation shows that the distance moved by a noble gas atom is directly proportional to the square root of the number of jumps and the length of each jump. As the temperature increases, the number of jumps and the length of each jump also increase, resulting in a greater distance traveled by the noble gas atoms.



The implications of noble gas diffusion for mantle dynamics are significant. The rate of diffusion is directly related to temperature, and as such, the diffusion of noble gases can provide insights into the thermal history of the Earth's mantle. By studying the diffusion of noble gases in mantle minerals, we can estimate the temperatures at which these minerals have been subjected to in the past. This information can then be used to reconstruct the thermal evolution of the Earth's mantle and understand the processes that have shaped it.



Furthermore, the diffusion of noble gases can also provide insights into the mixing and melting processes that occur in mantle plumes. As the mantle plume ascends towards the surface, it incorporates different materials from its surroundings, resulting in a unique geochemical signature. The presence of different noble gas isotopes in mantle plumes compared to mid-ocean ridge basalts (MORBs) is evidence of this mixing process. By studying the diffusion of noble gases in mantle plumes, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that have contributed to the chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle.



In conclusion, noble gas diffusion is a crucial process in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. By studying the diffusion of noble gases, we can gain valuable insights into the temperature history of the mantle and the processes that have shaped it. This information is essential for our understanding of the Earth's evolution and can help us make predictions about its future.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.4 Noble Gas Diffusion and its Implications for Mantle Dynamics:



### Subsection (optional): 5.4b Noble Gas Diffusion in the Mantle



Noble gas diffusion is a fundamental process that has played a crucial role in shaping the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. As mentioned in the previous section, noble gases are chemically inert and do not react with other elements, making them ideal tracers for studying the processes that have shaped our planet. In this section, we will explore the specific mechanisms of noble gas diffusion in the mantle and its implications for mantle dynamics.



In the Earth's mantle, noble gas diffusion occurs through the process of jump diffusion, where the atoms migrate from point vacancy to point vacancy by rapid, essentially random jumping. This movement can be described by the "random walk" model, where after a certain number of jumps, the atom will have moved an average distance. The frequency of jumps, denoted by T, is measured in jumps per second, and the average distance moved after n jumps of length α can be calculated using the equation:



$$

\Delta x = \sqrt{n} \alpha

$$



The diffusivity of noble gases in the mantle is influenced by several factors, including temperature, pressure, and the structure of the crystal lattice. In the crystal solid state, diffusion can occur through either interstitial or substitutional mechanisms, known as lattice diffusion. In interstitial lattice diffusion, atoms diffuse in between the lattice structure of another crystalline element, while in substitutional lattice diffusion, atoms can only move by substituting places with another atom. The prevalence of point vacancies in the crystal lattice, which increases with temperature according to the Arrhenius equation, also affects the rate of diffusion.



The diffusivity of noble gases in the mantle is also influenced by the composition of the mantle itself. Different minerals have different crystal structures and therefore different diffusion rates. For example, olivine, which is a major mineral in the upper mantle, has a higher diffusivity for noble gases compared to other minerals such as pyroxene and garnet. This means that noble gases will diffuse more quickly through olivine-rich regions of the mantle compared to other regions, which can have significant implications for mantle dynamics.



The diffusion of noble gases in the mantle has important implications for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By studying the distribution of noble gases in different regions of the mantle, we can gain insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. For example, the presence of high concentrations of noble gases in certain regions of the mantle can indicate the presence of ancient subducted material, providing evidence for plate tectonics and the recycling of material in the Earth's interior.



In conclusion, noble gas diffusion is a crucial process for understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. By studying the mechanisms and implications of noble gas diffusion, we can gain a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet and continue to shape it today. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 5: Noble Gas Constraints:



### Section: 5.4 Noble Gas Diffusion and its Implications for Mantle Dynamics:



### Subsection (optional): 5.4c Implications for Mantle Dynamics



In the previous section, we explored the mechanisms of noble gas diffusion in the Earth's mantle. Now, we will delve into the implications of this process for mantle dynamics.



Noble gas diffusion plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. As mentioned before, noble gases are chemically inert and do not react with other elements, making them ideal tracers for studying the processes that have shaped our planet. By studying the distribution and abundance of noble gases in the mantle, we can gain insights into the past and present dynamics of the Earth's interior.



One of the key implications of noble gas diffusion in the mantle is its role in mantle convection. Mantle convection is the process by which heat is transferred from the Earth's core to the surface, driving plate tectonics and other geodynamic processes. The movement of noble gases through the mantle can affect the distribution of heat and the rate of convection, ultimately shaping the Earth's surface and its evolution.



Furthermore, noble gas diffusion can also provide insights into the composition and origin of the Earth's mantle. Different noble gases have different diffusion rates, which can be used to trace the origin of mantle materials and their evolution over time. For example, the presence of helium-3 in the mantle can be used to trace the remnants of primordial material from the formation of the Earth, while the presence of argon-40 can indicate the presence of recycled crustal material.



In addition, noble gas diffusion can also provide information about the timing of mantle processes. By studying the diffusion rates of different noble gases, we can determine the age of mantle rocks and the timing of geological events such as subduction and volcanic eruptions. This information is crucial for understanding the history of the Earth's mantle and its ongoing evolution.



Overall, noble gas diffusion is a fundamental process that has shaped the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. Its implications for mantle dynamics are vast and continue to be a topic of research and exploration. By studying noble gas diffusion, we can gain a better understanding of the Earth's past, present, and future.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the role of noble gases in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. We have seen how the isotopic composition of noble gases can provide valuable insights into the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years. From the primordial noble gases trapped in ancient rocks to the radiogenic noble gases produced by the decay of radioactive elements, each type of noble gas has its own unique story to tell.



We have also discussed the various techniques used to measure noble gas isotopes and how these measurements can be used to constrain the timing and conditions of different geological events. By combining these techniques with other geochemical and geophysical data, we can build a more complete picture of the Earth's evolution and gain a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet.



Overall, the study of noble gases has greatly enhanced our understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. It has allowed us to unravel the complex history of our planet and shed light on the processes that have made it the unique and dynamic world that it is today.

- $^{40}K$/$^{39}K$ ratio in the sample = 0.0001

- Half-life of $^{40}K$ = 1.25 billion years

## Chapter: Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will explore the use of seismic tomography in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Seismic tomography is a technique that uses seismic waves to create images of the Earth's interior. By analyzing the speed and direction of these waves, we can gain insight into the composition and structure of the Earth's layers. This method has been crucial in advancing our understanding of the Earth's evolution, as it allows us to study the Earth's interior without physically accessing it. In this chapter, we will discuss the principles of seismic tomography, its applications in geology, and the insights it has provided into the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section 6.1: Constraints on Mantle Structure and Composition



Seismic tomography is a powerful tool that has revolutionized our understanding of the Earth's interior. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can create images of the Earth's interior and gain insight into its composition and structure. In this section, we will discuss the applications of seismic tomography in studying the mantle and how it has provided constraints on its structure and composition.



#### 6.1a: Seismic Tomography and Mantle Structure



Seismic tomography has been instrumental in revealing the complex structure of the Earth's mantle. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can create images of the mantle and identify variations in parameters such as anisotropy, anelasticity, density, and bulk sound velocity. These variations can be attributed to thermal or chemical differences, which are a result of processes such as mantle plumes, subducting slabs, and mineral phase changes.



One of the most significant applications of seismic tomography is in studying mantle plumes. The mantle plume hypothesis proposes that areas of volcanism not readily explained by plate tectonics, called hotspots, are a result of thermal upwelling from as deep as the core-mantle boundary. By using tomographic images, we can identify anomalies beneath hotspots, providing evidence for the existence of mantle plumes. These anomalies are often seen as large low-shear-velocity provinces, or superplumes, in the lower mantle, and are believed to reflect both thermal and compositional differences.



For example, the Yellowstone hotspot, responsible for volcanism at the Yellowstone Caldera, has been extensively studied using seismic tomography. The Yellowstone Geodynamic Project found a strong low-velocity body from ~30 to 250 km depth beneath Yellowstone, and a weaker anomaly from 250 to 650 km depth, which dipped 60° west-northwest. These features are attributed to the mantle plume beneath the hotspot being deflected eastward by flow in the upper mantle, as seen in S-wave models.



Another hotspot that has been studied using seismic tomography is the Hawaii hotspot, which produced the Hawaiian-Emperor seamount chain. Tomographic images have revealed a 500 to 600 km wide and up to 2,000 km deep anomaly, providing further evidence for the existence of mantle plumes.



In addition to studying hotspots, seismic tomography has also been crucial in understanding subduction zones. Subducting plates are colder than the mantle into which they are moving, creating a fast anomaly that is visible in tomographic images. By analyzing these images, we can gain insight into the structure and composition of subducting plates, providing valuable information about the processes occurring at subduction zones.



In conclusion, seismic tomography has been a game-changer in our understanding of the Earth's mantle. By providing detailed images of the mantle, it has allowed us to study its structure and composition without physically accessing it. Through its applications in studying hotspots and subduction zones, seismic tomography has provided valuable constraints on the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section 6.1: Constraints on Mantle Structure and Composition



Seismic tomography has revolutionized our understanding of the Earth's interior by providing detailed images of the mantle.
