# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section 6.1: Constraints on Mantle Structure and Composition



Seismic tomography has revolutionized our understanding of the Earth's interior by providing detailed images of the mantle. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can identify variations in parameters such as anisotropy, anelasticity, density, and bulk sound velocity. These variations can be attributed to thermal or chemical differences, which are a result of processes such as mantle plumes, subducting slabs, and mineral phase changes.



#### 6.1a: Seismic Tomography and Mantle Structure



One of the most significant applications of seismic tomography is in studying mantle plumes. The mantle plume hypothesis proposes that areas of volcanism not readily explained by plate tectonics, called hotspots, are a result of thermal upwelling from as deep as the core-mantle boundary. By using tomographic images, we can identify anomalies beneath hotspots, providing evidence for the existence of mantle plumes.



These anomalies are often seen as large low-shear-velocity provinces, or superplumes, in the lower mantle. These superplumes are believed to reflect both thermal and compositional differences, providing valuable insights into the chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle. For example, the Yellowstone hotspot, responsible for volcanism at the Yellowstone Caldera, has been extensively studied using seismic tomography. The Yellowstone Geodynamic Project found a strong low-velocity body from ~30 to 250 km depth beneath Yellowstone, and a weaker anomaly from 250 to 650 km depth which dipped 60° west-northwest. These features are attributed to the mantle plume beneath the hotspot being deflected eastward by flow in the upper mantle, as seen in S-wave models.



In addition to studying mantle plumes, seismic tomography has also been instrumental in understanding subduction zones. Subducting slabs can be identified in tomographic images as high-velocity anomalies, indicating colder and denser material sinking into the mantle. These images have provided evidence for the existence of subducted oceanic plates and have helped to refine our understanding of plate tectonics.



#### 6.1b: Seismic Tomography and Mantle Composition



In addition to providing insights into the structure of the mantle, seismic tomography has also been used to study the chemical composition of the Earth's interior. By analyzing the variations in seismic wave velocities, we can infer the presence of different minerals and their distribution within the mantle.



One example of this is the discovery of ultra-low velocity zones (ULVZs) in the lowermost mantle. These regions, characterized by extremely low seismic wave velocities, are believed to be composed of dense, iron-rich minerals such as bridgmanite and ferropericlase. The presence of these minerals suggests that the lowermost mantle may be more chemically heterogeneous than previously thought.



Another important application of seismic tomography in studying mantle composition is the identification of mantle plumes. As mentioned earlier, these plumes are believed to originate from the core-mantle boundary and bring up material from the deep mantle. By analyzing the seismic wave velocities in these regions, we can infer the composition of the material being brought up by the plumes. This has provided valuable insights into the chemical evolution of the Earth's mantle and the processes that have shaped it over time.



### Subsection 6.1c: Case Studies of Seismic Tomography



To further illustrate the capabilities of seismic tomography, let us examine two case studies: the Hawaiian hotspot and the Mariana Trench subduction zone.



The Hawaiian hotspot is one of the most well-studied hotspots in the world, and its mantle plume has been extensively imaged using seismic tomography. These images have revealed a strong low-velocity anomaly beneath the islands, extending from the surface to the core-mantle boundary. This anomaly is believed to be caused by a large, hot mantle plume that has been active for millions of years, resulting in the formation of the Hawaiian Islands.



In contrast, the Mariana Trench subduction zone is a region where the Pacific Plate is being subducted beneath the Philippine Plate. Seismic tomography has revealed a high-velocity anomaly beneath the Mariana Trench, indicating the presence of a subducted slab. This anomaly extends to a depth of over 1000 km, providing evidence for the existence of a deep subduction zone.



These case studies demonstrate the power of seismic tomography in providing detailed images of the Earth's interior and helping us to better understand its thermal and chemical evolution. As technology and data collection continue to improve, we can expect even more insights to be gained from this powerful tool.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section 6.2: Imaging Subduction Zones using Seismic Tomography



Subduction zones are one of the most dynamic and complex regions on Earth, where two tectonic plates converge and one plate is forced beneath the other into the mantle. This process plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth, as it recycles old oceanic crust and sediments back into the mantle. In this section, we will explore how seismic tomography has been used to image subduction zones and provide insights into their structure and dynamics.



#### 6.2a: Introduction to Subduction Zones



Subduction zones are characterized by a variety of geological features, including deep ocean trenches, volcanic arcs, and accretionary prisms. These features are a result of the complex interactions between the subducting slab and the overriding plate. However, the exact structure and dynamics of subduction zones are still not fully understood.



Seismic tomography has played a crucial role in advancing our understanding of subduction zones. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can identify variations in parameters such as anisotropy, anelasticity, density, and bulk sound velocity. These variations can be attributed to thermal or chemical differences, providing valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of subduction zones.



One of the most significant applications of seismic tomography in subduction zones is in studying the subducting slab. By imaging the subducting slab, we can identify its geometry, thickness, and velocity structure. This information can help us understand the processes that control the subduction of the slab, such as slab rollback and slab dehydration.



In addition to studying the subducting slab, seismic tomography has also been used to image the mantle wedge above the subducting slab. This region is characterized by high temperatures and partial melting, which can be imaged using seismic tomography. By studying the structure and dynamics of the mantle wedge, we can gain insights into the processes that drive arc volcanism and the formation of volcanic arcs.



Overall, seismic tomography has revolutionized our understanding of subduction zones and their role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By providing detailed images of the subducting slab and the mantle wedge, we can gain valuable insights into the complex processes that shape our planet. In the following sections, we will explore some specific examples of how seismic tomography has been used to image subduction zones and provide insights into their structure and dynamics.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section 6.2: Imaging Subduction Zones using Seismic Tomography



Subduction zones are one of the most dynamic and complex regions on Earth, where two tectonic plates converge and one plate is forced beneath the other into the mantle. This process plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth, as it recycles old oceanic crust and sediments back into the mantle. In this section, we will explore how seismic tomography has been used to image subduction zones and provide insights into their structure and dynamics.



#### 6.2a: Introduction to Subduction Zones



Subduction zones are characterized by a variety of geological features, including deep ocean trenches, volcanic arcs, and accretionary prisms. These features are a result of the complex interactions between the subducting slab and the overriding plate. However, the exact structure and dynamics of subduction zones are still not fully understood.



Seismic tomography has played a crucial role in advancing our understanding of subduction zones. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can identify variations in parameters such as anisotropy, anelasticity, density, and bulk sound velocity. These variations can be attributed to thermal or chemical differences, providing valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of subduction zones.



One of the most significant applications of seismic tomography in subduction zones is in studying the subducting slab. By imaging the subducting slab, we can identify its geometry, thickness, and velocity structure. This information can help us understand the processes that control the subduction of the slab, such as slab rollback and slab dehydration.



In addition to studying the subducting slab, seismic tomography has also been used to image the mantle wedge above the subducting slab. This region is characterized by high temperatures and partial melting, which can be imaged using seismic tomography. By studying the structure and dynamics of the mantle wedge, we can gain insights into the processes that drive subduction and the formation of volcanic arcs.



### 6.2b: Imaging Techniques for Subduction Zones



There are several imaging techniques used in seismic tomography to study subduction zones. One of the most commonly used techniques is P-wave tomography, which uses the speed of primary (P) waves to image the subsurface. P-waves travel faster through denser materials, allowing us to identify variations in density and composition within the subduction zone.



Another technique is S-wave tomography, which uses the speed of secondary (S) waves to image the subsurface. S-waves travel slower than P-waves and are more sensitive to anisotropy and anelasticity, making them useful for identifying variations in these parameters within the subduction zone.



A newer technique, called ambient noise tomography, uses the background seismic noise generated by ocean waves to image the subsurface. This technique has been particularly useful in imaging the shallow portions of subduction zones, where traditional seismic sources may not be effective.



Overall, these imaging techniques have greatly improved our understanding of subduction zones and their role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By providing detailed images of the subsurface, we can continue to unravel the complex processes that shape our planet. 





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section 6.2: Imaging Subduction Zones using Seismic Tomography



Subduction zones are one of the most dynamic and complex regions on Earth, where two tectonic plates converge and one plate is forced beneath the other into the mantle. This process plays a crucial role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth, as it recycles old oceanic crust and sediments back into the mantle. In this section, we will explore how seismic tomography has been used to image subduction zones and provide insights into their structure and dynamics.



#### 6.2a: Introduction to Subduction Zones



Subduction zones are characterized by a variety of geological features, including deep ocean trenches, volcanic arcs, and accretionary prisms. These features are a result of the complex interactions between the subducting slab and the overriding plate. However, the exact structure and dynamics of subduction zones are still not fully understood.



Seismic tomography has played a crucial role in advancing our understanding of subduction zones. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can identify variations in parameters such as anisotropy, anelasticity, density, and bulk sound velocity. These variations can be attributed to thermal or chemical differences, providing valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of subduction zones.



One of the most significant applications of seismic tomography in subduction zones is in studying the subducting slab. By imaging the subducting slab, we can identify its geometry, thickness, and velocity structure. This information can help us understand the processes that control the subduction of the slab, such as slab rollback and slab dehydration.



In addition to studying the subducting slab, seismic tomography has also been used to image the mantle wedge above the subducting slab. This region is characterized by high temperatures and partial melting, which can be imaged using seismic tomography. By studying the structure and dynamics of the mantle wedge, we can gain insights into the processes that drive subduction and the formation of volcanic arcs.



#### 6.2b: Imaging Techniques for Subduction Zones



There are several techniques used in seismic tomography to image subduction zones. One of the most commonly used methods is the travel time tomography, which involves measuring the travel time of seismic waves from earthquakes to seismic stations. By comparing the travel times of these waves, we can create a 3D image of the subsurface structure.



Another technique is the amplitude tomography, which uses the amplitude of seismic waves to image the subsurface structure. This method is particularly useful in imaging the low-velocity anomalies associated with subduction zones.



#### 6.2c: Recent Advances in Imaging Subduction Zones



Recent advances in seismic tomography have allowed for more detailed and accurate imaging of subduction zones. One such advancement is the use of ambient noise tomography, which utilizes the background seismic noise to create high-resolution images of the subsurface structure.



Another recent development is the use of full-waveform inversion, which involves modeling the entire waveform of seismic waves to create a more accurate image of the subsurface structure. This technique has been particularly useful in imaging the complex structures of subduction zones.



In addition to these advancements in imaging techniques, there have also been developments in data collection and processing methods, allowing for more comprehensive and detailed images of subduction zones.



Overall, the use of seismic tomography in imaging subduction zones has greatly advanced our understanding of these complex geological features. With continued advancements in technology and techniques, we can expect to gain even more insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth through the study of subduction zones.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section: 6.3 Mantle Plumes and their Seismic Signatures



### Subsection: 6.3a Understanding Mantle Plumes



Mantle plumes are one of the most intriguing and debated topics in geodynamics. These long, cylindrical structures are thought to originate from the core-mantle boundary and rise through the mantle, transporting heat and material to the Earth's surface. In this section, we will explore the concept of mantle plumes and their seismic signatures, as well as the ongoing debate surrounding their existence and role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.



#### 6.3a: Introduction to Mantle Plumes



Mantle plumes are believed to be responsible for the formation of hotspots, such as the one found in Iceland. These hotspots are characterized by high heat flow, volcanic activity, and unique geochemical signatures in the form of ocean island basalts (OIBs). The origin of mantle plumes is still a topic of debate, with two main hypotheses proposed: the plume hypothesis and the plate hypothesis.



According to the plume hypothesis, mantle plumes are narrow, hot upwellings that originate from the core-mantle boundary and rise through the mantle. As they reach the base of the lithosphere, they spread out and create a broad, hot region known as a "head." This head then melts the overlying lithosphere, forming a volcanic hotspot. The plume hypothesis also suggests that the near-surface material, such as subducted slabs and continental sediments, is transported down to the core-mantle boundary by subducting slabs and then brought back up to the surface by plumes.



On the other hand, the plate hypothesis proposes that subducted material is mostly re-circulated in the shallow mantle and tapped from there by volcanoes. This hypothesis suggests that the observed geochemical signatures in OIBs are a result of mixing between different mantle components, such as depleted mid-ocean ridge basalt (DMM) and enriched mantle (EM). In this context, mantle plumes are not necessary to explain the observed geochemical signatures.



#### 6.3b: Seismic Signatures of Mantle Plumes



One of the most significant pieces of evidence for the existence of mantle plumes comes from seismic tomography. By analyzing the speed and direction of seismic waves, we can identify variations in parameters such as anisotropy, anelasticity, density, and bulk sound velocity. These variations can be attributed to thermal or chemical differences, providing valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of mantle plumes.



In particular, seismic tomography has been used to image the stem of mantle plumes, which is the narrow, hot upwelling that originates from the core-mantle boundary. These images have revealed a region of low seismic velocity, indicating a higher temperature compared to the surrounding mantle. This is consistent with the plume hypothesis, which suggests that mantle plumes are hotter than the surrounding mantle.



Furthermore, seismic tomography has also been used to image the head of mantle plumes, which is the broad, hot region that spreads out at the base of the lithosphere. These images have shown a region of high seismic velocity, indicating a lower temperature compared to the stem of the plume. This is consistent with the idea that the head of the plume has cooled as it spreads out and melts the overlying lithosphere.



#### 6.3c: Ongoing Debate and Future Directions



Despite the evidence from seismic tomography, the existence and role of mantle plumes in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth is still a topic of debate. Some scientists argue that the observed seismic signatures can be explained by other processes, such as subduction or lithospheric thinning. Others argue that the geochemical signatures in OIBs can be explained by other mechanisms, such as mixing between different mantle components.



In order to further our understanding of mantle plumes, future research should focus on integrating different geophysical and geochemical data sets. This will allow for a more comprehensive exploration of mantle plumes and their role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. Additionally, continued advancements in seismic tomography techniques and technology will provide even more detailed images of mantle plumes, helping to resolve the ongoing debate surrounding their existence and significance.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section: 6.3 Mantle Plumes and their Seismic Signatures



### Subsection: 6.3b Seismic Signatures of Mantle Plumes



In the previous subsection, we discussed the concept of mantle plumes and their potential role in the formation of hotspots. In this section, we will explore the seismic signatures of mantle plumes and how they can be used to identify and study these structures.



#### 6.3b: Seismic Signatures of Mantle Plumes



One of the most commonly used methods to study mantle plumes is through seismic tomography. This technique involves using a network of seismometers to create three-dimensional images of the variation in seismic wave speed throughout the mantle. By mapping these variations, we can identify regions of lower seismic wave speeds, which are often associated with hotter mantle material.



However, interpreting these seismic wave speed anomalies can be challenging. While lower wave speeds are often attributed to hotter mantle material, they can also be caused by other factors such as composition and partial melt. This ambiguity makes it difficult to directly measure the temperature of mantle plumes using seismic tomography alone.



To overcome this challenge, scientists have developed more sophisticated approaches to analyze seismic data and distinguish between the effects of temperature, composition, and partial melt. These methods have allowed us to gain a better understanding of the seismic signatures of mantle plumes and their potential role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.



#### 6.3b: Seismic Tomography and the Debate Surrounding Mantle Plumes



While seismic tomography has provided valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the Earth's mantle, it has also sparked a debate surrounding the existence and role of mantle plumes. Some scientists argue that the observed seismic anomalies can be explained by other processes, such as subduction and plate tectonics, and that mantle plumes may not be necessary to explain the observed hotspots and OIBs.



On the other hand, proponents of the plume hypothesis argue that the unique geochemical signatures found in OIBs can only be explained by the presence of mantle plumes. They also point to evidence of deep-rooted, long-lived mantle structures that are consistent with the predictions of the plume hypothesis.



Despite this ongoing debate, seismic tomography remains a powerful tool for studying mantle plumes and their potential role in the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. As our understanding of seismic data and analysis techniques continues to improve, we may be able to gain a clearer picture of these enigmatic structures and their impact on our planet.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section: 6.3 Mantle Plumes and their Seismic Signatures



### Subsection: 6.3c Implications of Seismic Signatures



In the previous subsection, we discussed the concept of mantle plumes and their potential role in the formation of hotspots. We also explored the seismic signatures of mantle plumes and how they can be identified and studied using seismic tomography. In this section, we will delve deeper into the implications of these seismic signatures and their role in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.



#### 6.3c: Implications of Seismic Signatures



The identification and study of mantle plumes through seismic tomography have provided valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the Earth's mantle. By mapping the variations in seismic wave speeds, we can identify regions of lower wave speeds, which are often associated with hotter mantle material. This has allowed us to gain a better understanding of the distribution of heat within the Earth's interior and its potential role in shaping the planet's surface.



One of the key implications of these seismic signatures is the potential role of mantle plumes in the formation of hotspots. Hotspots are areas of intense volcanic activity that are not associated with plate boundaries. They are often found in the middle of tectonic plates, such as the Hawaiian Islands and Yellowstone National Park. Through seismic tomography, we have been able to identify regions of lower seismic wave speeds beneath these hotspots, suggesting the presence of mantle plumes.



The presence of mantle plumes and their associated hotspots has significant implications for the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. These structures are thought to be responsible for the formation of large igneous provinces (LIPs), which are massive volcanic eruptions that cover large areas of the Earth's surface. LIPs have been linked to major changes in the Earth's climate and have played a crucial role in the evolution of the planet's atmosphere and biosphere.



Furthermore, the study of mantle plumes and their seismic signatures has also shed light on the processes of mantle convection and plate tectonics. By understanding the distribution of heat within the Earth's interior, we can gain a better understanding of the forces that drive plate movement and the formation of new crust. This has important implications for the long-term evolution of the Earth's surface and the potential for future tectonic events.



#### 6.3c: The Debate Surrounding Mantle Plumes



While seismic tomography has provided valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the Earth's mantle, it has also sparked a debate surrounding the existence and role of mantle plumes. Some scientists argue that the observed seismic anomalies can be explained by other processes, such as compositional variations or partial melt, rather than the presence of mantle plumes.



This debate highlights the need for further research and a better understanding of the complexities of the Earth's interior. While seismic tomography has provided valuable information, it is just one piece of the puzzle in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of our planet. Continued advancements in technology and techniques will allow us to further explore and unravel the mysteries of the Earth's interior and its evolution over time.



In conclusion, the study of mantle plumes and their seismic signatures has provided valuable insights into the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. These structures have significant implications for the formation of hotspots, large igneous provinces, and the processes of mantle convection and plate tectonics. While there is still much to be discovered and debated, seismic tomography has played a crucial role in our understanding of the Earth's interior and its evolution over time.

## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section: 6.4 Seismic Anisotropy and its Relation to Mantle Flow



### Subsection: 6.4a Basics of Seismic Anisotropy



Seismic anisotropy is a powerful tool for understanding the structure and dynamics of the Earth's interior. It relies on the measurement of shear waves, which carry rich information about the properties of the Earth's materials. However, there are limitations to the use of seismic anisotropy, as it requires expensive 3-component geophones and is more effective for larger earthquakes.



One of the key applications of seismic anisotropy is in studying the anisotropy of the Earth's crust. This anisotropy can be caused by various factors, such as preferentially aligned joints or microcracks, layered bedding in sedimentary formations, or highly foliated metamorphic rocks. By studying the anisotropy of the crust, we can gain insights into the state of stress and changes in the stress field in active tectonic areas.



Both P-waves and S-waves can exhibit anisotropy, with P-waves showing a continuous dependence of velocity on the direction of propagation and S-waves showing a discrete dependence on the direction of polarization. This anisotropy can be decomposed into two polarization directions, providing valuable information about the properties of the Earth's materials.



In the context of mantle flow, seismic anisotropy can provide insights into the direction and speed of mantle flow. This is because the alignment of minerals in the mantle can cause anisotropy in the propagation of seismic waves. By mapping the variations in seismic wave speeds, we can identify regions of faster or slower mantle flow, which can help us understand the dynamics of the Earth's interior.



One of the key implications of seismic anisotropy in relation to mantle flow is the potential role of mantle plumes in the formation of hotspots. Hotspots are areas of intense volcanic activity that are not associated with plate boundaries. Through seismic tomography, we have been able to identify regions of lower seismic wave speeds beneath these hotspots, suggesting the presence of mantle plumes. This has provided valuable insights into the distribution of heat within the Earth's interior and its potential role in shaping the planet's surface.



In conclusion, seismic anisotropy is a powerful tool for understanding the structure and dynamics of the Earth's interior. By studying the anisotropy of the crust and mantle, we can gain valuable insights into the state of stress, changes in the stress field, and the direction and speed of mantle flow. This has significant implications for our understanding of the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section: 6.4 Seismic Anisotropy and its Relation to Mantle Flow



### Subsection: 6.4b Seismic Anisotropy and Mantle Flow



Seismic anisotropy is a powerful tool for understanding the structure and dynamics of the Earth's interior. It relies on the measurement of shear waves, which carry rich information about the properties of the Earth's materials. By studying the anisotropy of these waves, we can gain insights into the state of stress and changes in the stress field in active tectonic areas.



In the context of mantle flow, seismic anisotropy can provide valuable information about the direction and speed of mantle flow. This is because the alignment of minerals in the mantle can cause anisotropy in the propagation of seismic waves. By mapping the variations in seismic wave speeds, we can identify regions of faster or slower mantle flow, which can help us understand the dynamics of the Earth's interior.



One of the key implications of seismic anisotropy in relation to mantle flow is the potential role of mantle plumes in the formation of hotspots. Hotspots are areas of intense volcanism that are not readily explained by plate tectonics. The mantle plume hypothesis proposes that these hotspots are a result of thermal upwelling from as deep as the core-mantle boundary, which become diapirs in the crust. This theory is actively contested, but tomographic images have shown anomalies beneath some hotspots, providing evidence for the presence of mantle plumes.



The best imaged of these anomalies are large low-shear-velocity provinces, or superplumes, visible on S-wave models of the lower mantle. These superplumes are believed to reflect both thermal and compositional differences in the mantle. For example, the Yellowstone hotspot is responsible for volcanism at the Yellowstone Caldera and a series of extinct calderas along the Snake River Plain. The Yellowstone Geodynamic Project sought to image the plume beneath the hotspot and found a strong low-velocity body from ~30 to 250 km depth, and a weaker anomaly from 250 to 650 km depth which dipped 60° west-northwest. The authors attribute these features to the mantle plume beneath the hotspot being deflected eastward by flow in the upper mantle, as seen in S-wave models.



Another hotspot that has been extensively studied using seismic tomography is the Hawaii hotspot, which produced the Hawaiian–Emperor seamount chain. Tomographic images show it to be 500 to 600 km wide and up to 2,000 km deep. These images have provided valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the mantle beneath the hotspot, including the presence of a deep mantle plume.



In addition to hotspots, seismic anisotropy can also provide insights into mantle flow in subduction zones. Subducting plates are colder than the mantle into which they are moving, creating a fast anomaly that is visible in tomographic images. By studying the anisotropy of these images, we can gain a better understanding of the direction and speed of mantle flow in these regions.



Overall, seismic anisotropy is a powerful tool for studying the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By providing insights into mantle flow and the presence of mantle plumes, it has greatly contributed to our understanding of the Earth's interior and its dynamic processes. As technology and techniques continue to advance, we can expect even more detailed and accurate images of seismic anisotropy, further enhancing our knowledge of the Earth's evolution.





# Thermal and Chemical Evolution of the Earth: A Comprehensive Exploration



## Chapter 6: Seismic Tomography



### Section: 6.4 Seismic Anisotropy and its Relation to Mantle Flow



### Subsection: 6.4c Case Studies of Seismic Anisotropy



In the previous subsection, we discussed the general principles of seismic anisotropy and its relation to mantle flow. In this subsection, we will explore some specific case studies that demonstrate the power and potential of seismic anisotropy in understanding the Earth's interior.



One of the most well-known examples of seismic anisotropy is the Yellowstone hotspot, which we briefly mentioned in the last subsection. The Yellowstone hotspot is responsible for the formation of the Yellowstone Caldera and a series of extinct calderas along the Snake River Plain. The presence of a mantle plume beneath this region has been confirmed by tomographic images, which show a large low-shear-velocity province (LLSVP) beneath Yellowstone. This LLSVP is believed to be a result of both thermal and compositional differences in the mantle, providing evidence for the presence of a mantle plume.



Another interesting case study is the East African Rift System (EARS), which is a series of rift valleys extending from the Red Sea to Mozambique. The EARS is a unique example of continental rifting, where the lithosphere is being pulled apart, leading to the formation of new ocean basins. Seismic anisotropy studies in this region have revealed a complex pattern of mantle flow, with evidence of both upwelling and downwelling flow. This has helped us understand the dynamics of continental rifting and the formation of new ocean basins.



In addition to these large-scale features, seismic anisotropy can also provide insights into smaller-scale processes. For example, studies of seismic anisotropy in the upper mantle have revealed the presence of small-scale convection cells, which are believed to play a role in the mixing and transport of chemical elements in the mantle. These convection cells are also thought to contribute to the formation of hotspots and other geologic features.



Overall, these case studies demonstrate the power of seismic anisotropy in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. By mapping the variations in seismic wave speeds, we can gain insights into the dynamics of mantle flow and the processes that shape our planet. As technology and techniques continue to advance, we can expect even more exciting discoveries and insights from seismic anisotropy studies in the future.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the technique of seismic tomography and its applications in understanding the thermal and chemical evolution of the Earth. We have learned that seismic tomography uses seismic waves to create images of the Earth's interior, providing valuable information about its structure and composition. By analyzing the velocity of these waves, we can infer the temperature and density of different regions within the Earth. This has allowed us to gain a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet over billions of years.



We have also discussed the limitations of seismic tomography, such as the inability to image the Earth's core due to the high temperatures and pressures present. However, advancements in technology and data processing have allowed us to overcome some of these limitations and continue to improve our understanding of the Earth's interior.



Overall, seismic tomography has been a crucial tool in the study of the Earth's thermal and chemical evolution. It has provided us with valuable insights into the processes that have shaped our planet and will continue to be an essential technique in future research.
